      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the modern country near the Persian Gulf. For the former autonomous state in southern Arabia, see <a href="/wiki/Qu%27aiti" title="Qu'aiti">Qu'aiti</a>. For the city, see <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_City" title="Kuwait City">Kuwait City</a>.

      <p><b>Kuwait</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Kuwait.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/En-us-Kuwait.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Kuwait.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">الكويت</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-Kuwait</i></i>, <small>Gulf Arabic pronunciation:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ɪl‿ɪkweːt]</a></span> or <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[lɪkweːt]</a></span>), officially the <b>State of Kuwait</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">دولة الكويت</span>‎ <i><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar-State_of_Kuwait.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/69/Ar-State_of_Kuwait.oga" class="internal" title="Ar-State of Kuwait.oga">Dawlat al-Kuwait</a></span></span></i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>. Situated in the northern edge of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a> at the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, it borders <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq%E2%80%93Kuwait_border" title="Iraq–Kuwait border">the north</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kuwait%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_border" title="Kuwait–Saudi Arabia border">the south</a>. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Kuwait has a population of 4.5&nbsp;million people: 1.3 million are Kuwaitis and 3.2 million are expatriates.<sup id="cite_ref-census_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Expatriates_in_Kuwait" title="Expatriates in Kuwait">Expatriates</a> account for approximately 70% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">Oil reserves</a> were discovered in commercial quantities in 1938. In 1946, crude oil was exported for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> From 1946 to 1982, the country underwent large-scale modernization. In the 1980s, Kuwait experienced a period of geopolitical instability and an economic crisis following the <a href="/wiki/Souk_Al-Manakh_stock_market_crash" title="Souk Al-Manakh stock market crash">stock market crash</a>. In 1990, Kuwait was <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" title="Invasion of Kuwait">invaded</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_Governorate" title="Kuwait Governorate">annexed</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq" title="Ba'athist Iraq">Saddam's Iraq</a>. The Iraqi occupation of Kuwait came to an end in 1991 after <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">military intervention</a> by a military coalition led by the United States. Kuwait is a non-NATO ally of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Kuwait is a constitutional <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Semi-democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-democracy">semi-democratic</a> political system. Kuwait has a <a href="/wiki/World_Bank_high-income_economy" title="World Bank high-income economy">World Bank high-income economy</a> backed by the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_proven_oil_reserves#Countries" title="List of countries by proven oil reserves">sixth largest oil reserves</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_dinar" title="Kuwaiti dinar">Kuwaiti dinar</a> is the highest valued currency in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-curr_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-curr-14">[14]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, the country has the nineteenth <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">highest per capita income</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kuwait" title="Constitution of Kuwait">Constitution</a> was <a href="/wiki/Promulgation" title="Promulgation">promulgated</a> in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-ras_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ras-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rsf_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsf-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsjo_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjo-18">[18]</a></sup> Kuwait is home to the largest <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Jaber_Al-Ahmad_Cultural_Centre" title="Sheikh Jaber Al-Ahmad Cultural Centre">opera house</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_National_Cultural_District" title="Kuwait National Cultural District">Kuwait National Cultural District</a> is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Global_Cultural_Districts_Network" title="Global Cultural Districts Network">Global Cultural Districts Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gcdn_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcdn-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Kuwait" title="History of Kuwait">History of Kuwait</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> (6500 BC), northern Kuwait was the central site of interaction between the peoples of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-subiya_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subiya-23">[23]</a></sup> mainly centered in <a href="/wiki/Bahra_1" title="Bahra 1">As-Subiya</a> in northern Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The earliest evidence of human habitation in Kuwait dates back 8000 B.C. where <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> tools were found in Burgan.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesopotamian">Mesopotamians</a> first settled in the Kuwaiti island of <a href="/wiki/Failaka_Island" title="Failaka Island">Failaka</a> in 2000 B.C.<sup id="cite_ref-t_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-t-28">[28]</a></sup> Traders from the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> inhabited Failaka and ran a mercantile business.<sup id="cite_ref-t_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-t-28">[28]</a></sup> The island had many Mesopotamian-style buildings typical of those found in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> dating from around 2000 B.C.<sup id="cite_ref-t_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-t-28">[28]</a></sup> The Neolithic inhabitants of Kuwait were among the world's earliest maritime traders.<sup id="cite_ref-se_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-se-29">[29]</a></sup> One of the world's earliest reed-boats was discovered in northern Kuwait dating back to the Ubaid period.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 4000 BC until 2000 BC, the bay of Kuwait was home to the <a href="/wiki/Dilmun_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Dilmun civilization">Dilmun civilization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ak_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ak-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Dilmun's control of the bay of Kuwait included Kuwait City's <a href="/wiki/Shuwaikh_Port" title="Shuwaikh Port">Shuwaikh Port</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Akkaz_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkaz Island">Akkaz Island</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-ak_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ak-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Umm_an_Namil_Island" title="Umm an Namil Island">Umm an Namil Island</a><sup id="cite_ref-ak_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ak-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-akkaz_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-akkaz-34">[34]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Failaka_Island" title="Failaka Island">Failaka Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ak_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ak-31">[31]</a></sup> At its peak in 2000 BC, the Dilmun empire controlled the trade routes from Mesopotamia to India and the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a>. Dilmun's commercial power began to decline after 1800 BC. Piracy flourished throughout the region during Dilmun's decline. After 600 BC, the Babylonians added Dilmun to their empire. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:HistoricCoins.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HistoricCoins.jpg/220px-HistoricCoins.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HistoricCoins.jpg/330px-HistoricCoins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HistoricCoins.jpg/440px-HistoricCoins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="378"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:HistoricCoins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ancient coins found on <a href="/wiki/Failaka_Island" title="Failaka Island">Failaka Island</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the Dilmun era (from ca. 3000 BC), Failaka was known as "<a href="/wiki/Agarum" title="Agarum">Agarum</a>", the land of <a href="/wiki/Enzak" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzak">Enzak</a>, a great god in the Dilmun civilization according to Sumerian cuneiform texts found on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-is_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-is-35">[35]</a></sup> As part of Dilmun, Failaka became a hub for the civilization from the end of the 3rd to the middle of the 1st millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-is_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-is-35">[35]</a></sup> Failaka was settled following 2000 BC after a drop in sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> After the Dilmun civilization, Failaka was inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-kas_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kas-37">[37]</a></sup> and was formally under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Kassites#Kassite_Dynasty_of_Babylon" title="Kassites">Kassite dynasty of Babylon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kas_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kas-37">[37]</a></sup> Studies indicate traces of human settlement can be found on Failaka dating back to as early as the end of the 3rd millennium BC, and extending until the 20th century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-is_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-is-35">[35]</a></sup> Many of the artifacts found in Falaika are linked to Mesopotamian civilizations and seem to show that Failaka was gradually drawn toward the civilization based in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II" title="Nebuchadnezzar II">Nebuchadnezzar II</a>, Failaka was under <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> control.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Cuneiform documents found in Failaka indicate the presence of Babylonians in the island's population.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Kings_of_Babylon" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Kings of Babylon">Babylonian Kings</a> were present in Failaka during the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a> period, <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a> had a governor in Failaka and Nebuchadnezzar II had a palace and temple in Falaika.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sh_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sh-42">[42]</a></sup> Failaka also contained temples dedicated to the worship of <a href="/wiki/Shamash" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>, the Mesopotamian sun god in the Babylonian pantheon.<sup id="cite_ref-sh_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sh-42">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 4th century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greeks">ancient Greeks</a> colonized the bay of Kuwait under <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, the ancient Greeks named mainland Kuwait <i>Larissa</i> and Failaka was named <i><a href="/wiki/Ikaros_(Failaka_Island)" title="Ikaros (Failaka Island)">Ikaros</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a>, Alexander the Great named Failaka <i>Ikaros</i> because it resembled the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_island" class="mw-redirect" title="Aegean island">Aegean island</a> of that name in size and shape. Some elements of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a> were mixed with the local cults in Failaka.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> "Ikaros" was also the name of a prominent city situated in Failaka.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Remains of Greek colonization include a large <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Towns_of_ancient_Greece#Military_settlements" title="Towns of ancient Greece">fort</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek temple">Greek temples</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 127 BC, the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Characene" title="Characene">Characene</a> was established around the Bay of Kuwait. Characene was centered in the region encompassing southern Mesopotamia,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> including Failaka island.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> A busy Parthian commercial station existed on Failaka island.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The earliest recorded mention of Kuwait was in 150 AD in the geographical treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i> by Greek scholar <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sa_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sa-53">[53]</a></sup> Ptolemy mentioned the Bay of Kuwait as <i>Hieros Kolpos</i> (<i>Sacer Sinus</i> in the Latin versions).<sup id="cite_ref-sa_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sa-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 224 AD, Kuwait became part of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a>. At the time of the Sassanid Empire, Kuwait was known as <i>Meshan</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> which was an alternative name of the kingdom of Characene.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Akkaz was a <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Partho</a>-<a href="/wiki/Sassanian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanian">Sassanian</a> site; the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Sassanid religion</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_silence" class="mw-redirect" title="Tower of silence">tower of silence</a> was discovered in northern Akkaz.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-akkaz_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-akkaz-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-takkaz_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-takkaz-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In addition to <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Partho</a>-Sasanian settlements, Akkaz also contained Christian settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-takkaz_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-takkaz-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-christian_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-christian-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-akkaz_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-akkaz-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Nestorian" class="mw-redirect" title="Nestorian">Nestorian</a> settlements flourished in Akkaz and Failaka from the 5th century until the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-nes_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nes-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-christian_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-christian-61">[61]</a></sup> Excavations have revealed several farms, villages and two large churches dating from the 5th and 6th century.<sup id="cite_ref-nes_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nes-62">[62]</a></sup> Archaeologists are currently excavating nearby sites to understand the extent of the settlements that flourished in the eighth and ninth centuries A.D.<sup id="cite_ref-nes_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nes-62">[62]</a></sup> An old island tradition is that a community grew up around a Christian mystic and hermit.<sup id="cite_ref-nes_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nes-62">[62]</a></sup> The small farms and villages were eventually abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-nes_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nes-62">[62]</a></sup> Remains of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> era Nestorian churches were found at Al-Qusur in Failaka. Pottery at the site can be dated from as early as the first half of the 7th century through the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 636 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chains" title="Battle of Chains">Battle of Chains</a> between the Sassanid Empire and <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> was fought in Kuwait near the town of <a href="/wiki/Kazma" title="Kazma">Kazma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-trade_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trade-66">[66]</a></sup> At the time, Kuwait was under the control of the Sassanid Empire. The Battle of Chains was the first battle of the Rashidun Caliphate in which the Muslim army sought to extend its frontiers. </p>
      <p>As a result of Rashidun victory in 636 AD, the bay of Kuwait was home to the city of <a href="/wiki/Kazma" title="Kazma">Kazma</a> (also known as "Kadhima" or "Kāzimah") in the early Islamic era.<sup id="cite_ref-trade_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trade-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-academia_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academia-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jstor_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor-72">[72]</a></sup> Medieval Arabic sources contain multiple references to the bay of Kuwait in the early Islamic period.<sup id="cite_ref-academia_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academia-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jstor_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The city functioned as a <a href="/wiki/Port_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Port city">trade port</a> and resting place for pilgrims on their way from Iraq to Hejaz. The city was controlled by the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Al-Hirah" title="Al-Hirah">Al-Hirah</a> in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-academia_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-academia-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In the early Islamic period, the bay of Kuwait was known for being a fertile area.<sup id="cite_ref-trade_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trade-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Kazma" title="Kazma">Kuwaiti city of Kazma</a> was a stop for caravans coming from <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> and Mesopotamia en route to the Arabian Peninsula. The poet <a href="/wiki/Al-Farazdaq" title="Al-Farazdaq">Al-Farazdaq</a> was born in the Kuwaiti city of Kazma.<sup id="cite_ref-kuwa_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuwa-78">[78]</a></sup> Al-Farazdaq is recognized as one of the greatest <a href="/wiki/Classical_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical literature">classical poets</a> of the Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-kuwa_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuwa-78">[78]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="1521.E2.80.931946:_Founding_of_modern_Kuwait"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1521–1946:_Founding_of_modern_Kuwait">1521–1946: Founding of modern Kuwait</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1521–1946: Founding of modern Kuwait">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 1521, Kuwait was under <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a> control.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In the late 16th century, the Portuguese built a defensive settlement in Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In 1613, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_City" title="Kuwait City">Kuwait City</a> was founded as a <a href="/wiki/Fishing_village" title="Fishing village">fishing village</a> predominantly populated by fishermen. Administratively, it was a sheikhdom, ruled by local <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikhs</a> from <a href="/wiki/Bani_Khalid" title="Bani Khalid">Bani Khalid</a> clan.<sup id="cite_ref-casey_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casey-81">[81]</a></sup> In 1682 or 1716, the <a href="/wiki/Bani_Utbah" title="Bani Utbah">Bani Utbah</a> settled in Kuwait City, which at this time was still inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Fisherman" title="Fisherman">fishermen</a> and primarily functioned as a fishing village under Bani Khalid control.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bani_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bani-83">[83]</a></sup> Sometime after the death of the Bani Khalid's leader Barrak Bin Urair and the fall of the Bani Khalid Emirate, the Utub were able to wrest control of Kuwait as a result of successive <a href="/wiki/Marriage_of_state" title="Marriage of state">matrimonial alliances</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bani_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bani-83">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the early eighteenth century, Kuwait prospered as a maritime <a href="/wiki/Port_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Port city">port city</a> and rapidly became the principal commercial center for the transit of goods between <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscat" title="Muscat">Muscat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sail_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sail-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> By the mid 1700s, Kuwait had already established itself as the major trading route from the Persian Gulf to <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kw_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kw-86">[86]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Persian_War_(1775%E2%80%931776)" title="Ottoman–Persian War (1775–1776)">Persian siege of Basra in 1775–79</a>, Iraqi merchants took refuge in Kuwait and were partly instrumental in the expansion of Kuwait's boat-building and trading activities.<sup id="cite_ref-boom_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boom-87">[87]</a></sup> As a result, Kuwait's maritime commerce boomed,<sup id="cite_ref-boom_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boom-87">[87]</a></sup> as the Indian trade routes with Baghdad, Aleppo, <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> were diverted to Kuwait during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-kw_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kw-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> was diverted to Kuwait in 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-eas_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eas-89">[89]</a></sup> The East India Company secured the sea routes between Kuwait, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and the east coasts of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-eas_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eas-89">[89]</a></sup> After the Persians withdrew from <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> in 1779, Kuwait continued to attract trade away from Basra.<sup id="cite_ref-mer_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mer-90">[90]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between the years 1775 and 1779, the Indian trade routes with Baghdad, Aleppo, <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> were diverted to Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-kw_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kw-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> was diverted to Kuwait in 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-eas_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eas-89">[89]</a></sup> The East India Company secured the sea routes between Kuwait, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and the east coasts of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-eas_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eas-89">[89]</a></sup> After the Persians withdrew from Basra in 1779, Kuwait continued to attract trade away from Basra.<sup id="cite_ref-mer_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mer-90">[90]</a></sup>The flight of many of Basra's leading merchants to Kuwait continued to play a significant role in Basra's commercial stagnation well into the 1850s.<sup id="cite_ref-mer_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mer-90">[90]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg/220px-MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg/330px-MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg/440px-MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MarinetimeMKuwaitAlshami.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Al-Hashemi-II" title="Al-Hashemi-II">Marine Museum</a> in Kuwait City. Demonstrates the founding of Kuwait as a sea port for merchants.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Basra's instability helped foster economic prosperity in Kuwait in the second half of the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-jasser_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jasser-92">[92]</a></sup> Kuwait became prosperous due to Basra's instability in the late 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-w_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w-93">[93]</a></sup> In the late 18th century, Kuwait partly functioned as a haven for Basra's merchants fleeing <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman government">Ottoman government</a> persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-basra_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basra-94">[94]</a></sup> Kuwait was the center of <a href="/wiki/Boat_building" title="Boat building">boat building</a> in the Persian Gulf region.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Kuwaiti ship vessels were renowned throughout the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-al_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-al-97">[97]</a></sup> Kuwaitis also developed a reputation as the best <a href="/wiki/Sailor" title="Sailor">sailors</a> in the Persian Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-sail_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sail-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In the 19th century, Kuwait became significant in the <a href="/wiki/Horse_trading" title="Horse trading">horse trade</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-hor_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hor-100">[100]</a></sup> horses were regularly shipped by the way of sailing boats from Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-hor_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hor-100">[100]</a></sup> In the mid 19th century, it was estimated that Kuwait was exporting an average of 800 horses to India annually.<sup id="cite_ref-jasser_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jasser-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1890s, Kuwait was threatened by the Ottoman Empire. In a bid to address its security issues, ruler Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Mubarak_Al-Sabah" title="Mubarak Al-Sabah">Mubarak Al Sabah</a> signed an agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">British government in India</a>, subsequently known as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Kuwaiti_Agreement_of_1899" title="Anglo-Kuwaiti Agreement of 1899">Anglo-Kuwaiti Agreement of 1899</a> and became a <a href="/wiki/British_protectorate" title="British protectorate">British protectorate</a>. This gave Britain exclusivity of access and trade with Kuwait, and excluded Iraq to the north from a port on the Persian Gulf. The <a href="/wiki/Sheikhdom_of_Kuwait" title="Sheikhdom of Kuwait">Sheikhdom of Kuwait</a> remained a British protectorate from 1899 to 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-casey_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casey-81">[81]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Mubarak_Al-Sabah" title="Mubarak Al-Sabah">reign of Mubarak</a>, Kuwait was dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Marseilles" class="mw-redirect" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a> of the Persian Gulf" because its economic vitality attracted a large variety of people.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ara_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ara-102">[102]</a></sup> The population was cosmopolitan and ethnically diverse, including Arabs, Persians, Africans, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Kuwait" title="History of the Jews in Kuwait">Jews</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Kuwait#History" title="Armenians in Kuwait">Armenians</a>. Kuwait was known for its <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pa_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pa-103">[103]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the first decades of the twentieth century, Kuwait had a well-established elite: wealthy trading families who were linked by marriage and shared economic interests.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> The elite were long-settled, urban families, the majority of which claim descent from the original 30 Bani Utubi families.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> The wealthiest families were trade merchants who acquired their wealth from long-distance commerce, shipbuilding and pearling.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> They were a cosmopolitan elite, they traveled extensively to India, Africa and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> The elite educated their sons abroad more than other Gulf Arab elite.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> Western visitors noted that the Kuwaiti elite used European office systems, <a href="/wiki/Typewriters" class="mw-redirect" title="Typewriters">typewriters</a> and followed <a href="/wiki/European_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="European culture">European culture</a> with curiosity.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> The richest families were involved in general trade.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> The Kuwaiti merchant families of Al-Ghanim and Al-Hamad were estimated to be worth <a href="/wiki/Millionaire" title="Millionaire">millions</a> before the 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-elite_104-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elite-104">[104]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1919, Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Salim_Al-Mubarak_Al-Sabah" title="Salim Al-Mubarak Al-Sabah">Salim Al-Mubarak Al-Sabah</a> intended to build a commercial city in the south of Kuwait. This caused a diplomatic crisis with Najd, but Britain intervened, discouraging Sheikh Salim. In 1920, an attempt by the <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Ikhwan</a> to build a stronghold in southern Kuwait led to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hamdh" title="Battle of Hamdh">Battle of Hamdh</a>. The Battle of Hamdh involved 2,000 <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Ikhwan</a> fighters against 100 Kuwaiti <a href="/wiki/Cavalrymen" class="mw-redirect" title="Cavalrymen">cavalrymen</a> and 200 Kuwaiti <a href="/wiki/Infantrymen" class="mw-redirect" title="Infantrymen">infantrymen</a>. The battle lasted for six days and resulted in heavy but unknown casualties on both sides resulting in the victory of the Ikhwan forces and leading to the battle of Jahra around the Kuwait Red Fort. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jahra" title="Battle of Jahra">Battle of Jahra</a> happened as the result of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hamdh" title="Battle of Hamdh">Battle of Hamdh</a>. A force of three to four thousand <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Ikhwan</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Faisal_Al-Dawish" class="mw-redirect" title="Faisal Al-Dawish">Faisal Al-Dawish</a>, attacked the <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_Red_Fort" title="Kuwait Red Fort">Red Fort</a> at Al-Jahra, defended by fifteen hundred men. The fort was besieged and the Kuwaiti position precarious; had the fort fallen, Kuwait would likely have been incorporated into Ibn Saud's empire.<sup id="cite_ref-toth_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-toth-105">[105]</a></sup> The Ikhwan attack repulsed for the while, negotiations began between Salim and Al-Dawish; the latter threatened another attack if the Kuwaiti forces did not surrender. The local merchant class convinced Salim to call in help from British troops, who showed up with airplanes and three warships, ending the attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-toth_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-toth-105">[105]</a></sup> After the Battle of Jahra, Ibn Saud's warriors, the <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Ikhwan</a>, demanded that Kuwait follows five rules: evict all the <a href="/wiki/Shias" class="mw-redirect" title="Shias">Shias</a>, adopt the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Ikhwan doctrine</a>, label the Turks "<a href="/wiki/Heretics" class="mw-redirect" title="Heretics">heretics</a>", abolish smoking, <a href="/wiki/Munkar_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Munkar (disambiguation)">munkar</a> and prostitution, and destroy the American missionary hospital.<sup id="cite_ref-bassem_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bassem-106">[106]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Kuwait%E2%80%93Najd_War" title="Kuwait–Najd War">Kuwait–Najd War</a> of 1919–20 erupted in the <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_I" title="Aftermath of World War I">aftermath of World War I</a>. The war occurred because <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud" title="Ibn Saud">Ibn Saud</a> of Najd wanted to annex Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-block_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-block-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-asa_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asa-108">[108]</a></sup> The sharpened conflict between Kuwait and Najd led to the death of hundreds of Kuwaitis. The war resulted in sporadic border clashes throughout 1919–1920. Following the Kuwait–Najd War in 1919–20, Ibn Saud imposed a trade blockade against Kuwait from the years 1923 until 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-do_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-109">[109]</a></sup> The goal of the Saudi economic and military attacks on Kuwait was to annex as much of Kuwait's territory as possible. At the <a href="/wiki/Uqair_Protocol_of_1922" title="Uqair Protocol of 1922">Uqair conference</a> in 1922, the boundaries of Kuwait and Najd were set; as a result of British interference, Kuwait had no representative at the <a href="/wiki/Uqair_Protocol_of_1922" title="Uqair Protocol of 1922">Uqair conference</a>. After the Uqair conference, Kuwait was still subjected to a Saudi economic blockade and intermittent Saudi <a href="/wiki/Raid_(military)" title="Raid (military)">raiding</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kuwait1944.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Kuwait1944.jpg/250px-Kuwait1944.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Kuwait1944.jpg/375px-Kuwait1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Kuwait1944.jpg/500px-Kuwait1944.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="497"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kuwait1944.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Celebration at <a href="/wiki/Seif_Palace" title="Seif Palace">Seif Palace</a> in 1944
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> harmed Kuwait's economy, starting in the late 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-do_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-109">[109]</a></sup> International trading was one of Kuwait's main sources of income before oil.<sup id="cite_ref-do_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-109">[109]</a></sup> Kuwaiti merchants were mostly intermediary merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-do_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-109">[109]</a></sup> As a result of the decline of European demand for goods from India and Africa, Kuwait's economy suffered. The decline in international trade resulted in an increase in gold smuggling by Kuwaiti ships to India.<sup id="cite_ref-do_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-109">[109]</a></sup> Some Kuwaiti merchant families became rich from this smuggling.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-110">[110]</a></sup> Kuwait's <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearl</a> industry also collapsed as a result of the worldwide economic depression.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-110">[110]</a></sup> At its height, Kuwait's pearl industry had led the world's luxury market, regularly sending out between 750 and 800 ships to meet the European elite's desire for pearls.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-110">[110]</a></sup> During the economic depression, luxuries like pearls were in little demand.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-110">[110]</a></sup> The Japanese invention of <a href="/wiki/Cultured_pearl" title="Cultured pearl">cultured pearls</a> also contributed to the collapse of Kuwait's pearl industry.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-110">[110]</a></sup> </p>
      <p> In 1937, <a href="/wiki/Freya_Stark" title="Freya Stark">Freya Stark</a> wrote about the extent of poverty in Kuwait at the time:<sup id="cite_ref-do_109-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-109">[109]</a></sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Poverty has settled in Kuwait more heavily since my last visit five years ago, both by sea, where the pearl trade continues to decline, and by land, where the blockade established by Saudi Arabia now harms the merchants.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Some merchant families left Kuwait in the early 1930s due to the prevalence of economic hardship caused by Saudi Arabia's economic blockade against Kuwait. At the time of the discovery of oil in 1936–1937, most of Kuwait's inhabitants were impoverished. </p>
      <p>Throughout the 1930s, Kuwaiti people opposed the British imposed separation of Kuwait from Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup> In 1938, the "Free Kuwaiti Movement" was established by Kuwaiti youth who opposed British rule and submitted a petition demanding the Iraqi government reunifies Kuwait and Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup> Due to fears of armed uprising in Kuwait, the Al Sabah agreed to the establishment of a legislative council to represent the "Free Kuwaiti Movement" demanding the reunification of Iraq and Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In 1938, the council's first meeting resulted in unanimous resolutions demanding the reunification of Kuwait and Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In March 1939, a popular armed uprising erupted within Kuwait to reunify with Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup> The Al Sabah family, along with British military support, violently put down the uprising, and killed and imprisoned its participants.<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup> King Ghazi of Iraq publicly demanded the release of the Kuwaiti prisoners and warned the Al Sabah family to end the repression of the "Free Kuwaiti Movement".<sup id="cite_ref-fkm_111-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fkm-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="1946.E2.80.931982:_Golden_Era_of_Kuwait"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1946–1982:_Golden_Era_of_Kuwait">1946–1982: Golden Era of Kuwait</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1946–1982: Golden Era of Kuwait">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Between 1946–1982, Kuwait experienced a period of prosperity driven by oil and its liberal atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-venezia_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-venezia-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pavilion_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pavilion-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-farid_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-farid-116">[116]</a></sup> In popular discourse, the years between 1946 and 1982 are referred to as the "Golden Era of Kuwait".<sup id="cite_ref-venezia_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-venezia-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pavilion_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pavilion-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-farid_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-farid-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 1950, a major public-work programme began to enable Kuwaitis to enjoy a modern standard of living. By 1952, the country became the largest oil exporter in the Persian Gulf region. This massive growth attracted many foreign workers, especially from Palestine, India, and Egypt – with the latter being particularly political within the context of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Cold_War" title="Arab Cold War">Arab Cold War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In June 1961, Kuwait became independent with the end of the <a href="/wiki/Sheikhdom_of_Kuwait#History_as_a_Protected_State_of_Britain" title="Sheikhdom of Kuwait">British protectorate</a> and the Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Al-Salim_Al-Sabah" title="Abdullah Al-Salim Al-Sabah">Abdullah Al-Salim Al-Sabah</a> became <a href="/wiki/Emir_of_Kuwait" title="Emir of Kuwait">Emir of Kuwait</a>. Kuwait's <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Kuwait" title="Public holidays in Kuwait">national day</a>, however, is celebrated on 25 February, the anniversary of the coronation of Sheikh Abdullah (it was originally celebrated on 19 June, the date of independence, but concerns over the summer heat caused the government to move it).<sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2004_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2004-119">[119]</a></sup> Under the terms of the newly drafted <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kuwait" title="Constitution of Kuwait">Constitution</a>, Kuwait held its first <a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_parliamentary_election,_1963" class="mw-redirect" title="Kuwaiti parliamentary election, 1963">parliamentary elections in 1963</a>. Kuwait was the first of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Arab states of the Persian Gulf</a> to establish a constitution and parliament. </p>
      <p>In the 1960s and 1970s, Kuwait was considered the most developed country in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-index_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index-122">[122]</a></sup> Kuwait was the pioneer in the Middle East in diversifying its earnings away from oil exports.<sup id="cite_ref-swf_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-swf-123">[123]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_Investment_Authority" title="Kuwait Investment Authority">Kuwait Investment Authority</a> is the world's first sovereign wealth fund. From the 1970s onward, Kuwait scored highest of all Arab countries on the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-index_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_University" title="Kuwait University">Kuwait University</a> was established in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-index_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index-122">[122]</a></sup> Kuwait's <a href="#Theatre">theatre industry</a> was well known throughout the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-venezia_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-venezia-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-index_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index-122">[122]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1960s and 1970s, Kuwait's press was described as one of the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freest in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-review_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-review-124">[124]</a></sup> Kuwait was the pioneer in the literary renaissance in the Arab region.<sup id="cite_ref-pioneer_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pioneer-125">[125]</a></sup> In 1958, <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Arabi_(magazine)" title="Al-Arabi (magazine)">Al-Arabi</a></i> magazine was first published. The magazine went on to become the most popular magazine in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-pioneer_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pioneer-125">[125]</a></sup> Many Arab writers moved to Kuwait because they enjoyed greater <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of expression">freedom of expression</a> than elsewhere in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-newsmedia_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsmedia-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The Iraqi poet <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Matar" title="Ahmed Matar">Ahmed Matar</a> left Iraq in the 1970s to take refuge in the more liberal environment of Kuwait. </p>
      <p>Kuwaiti society embraced liberal and non-traditional attitudes throughout the 1960s and 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> For example, most Kuwaiti women did not wear the <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a> in the 1960s and 70s.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1982_to_present">1982 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1982 to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jacckuwait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Jacckuwait.jpg/220px-Jacckuwait.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Jacckuwait.jpg/330px-Jacckuwait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Jacckuwait.jpg/440px-Jacckuwait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="490" data-file-height="367"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jacckuwait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Jaber_Al-Ahmad_Cultural_Centre" title="Sheikh Jaber Al-Ahmad Cultural Centre">Sheikh Jaber Al-Ahmad Cultural Centre</a><sup id="cite_ref-alb_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alb-131">[131]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the early 1980s, Kuwait experienced a major <a href="/wiki/Economic_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic crisis">economic crisis</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Souk_Al-Manakh_stock_market_crash" title="Souk Al-Manakh stock market crash">Souk Al-Manakh stock market crash</a> and <a href="/wiki/1980s_oil_glut" title="1980s oil glut">decrease in oil price</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a>, Kuwait supported Iraq. Throughout the 1980s, there were several <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_in_Kuwait" title="Terrorism in Kuwait">terror attacks</a> in Kuwait, including the <a href="/wiki/1983_Kuwait_bombings" title="1983 Kuwait bombings">1983 Kuwait bombings</a>, hijacking of <a href="/wiki/1983_Kuwait_bombings#Aircraft_hijackings_(1984–1988)" title="1983 Kuwait bombings">several Kuwait Airways planes</a> and the attempted assassination of <a href="/wiki/Jaber_Al-Ahmad_Al-Sabah" title="Jaber Al-Ahmad Al-Sabah">Emir Jaber</a> in 1985. Kuwait was a regional hub of science and technology in the 1960s and 1970s up until the early 1980s; the scientific research sector significantly suffered due to the terror attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-s&amp;t_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s&amp;t-133">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the Iran–Iraq War ended, Kuwait declined an Iraqi request to forgive its US$65&nbsp;billion debt.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> An economic rivalry between the two countries ensued after Kuwait increased its oil production by 40 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory2004_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory2004-135">[135]</a></sup> Tensions between the two countries increased further in July 1990, after Iraq complained to <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> claiming that Kuwait was stealing its oil from a field near the border by <a href="/wiki/Slant_drilling" class="mw-redirect" title="Slant drilling">slant drilling</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Rumaila_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Rumaila field">Rumaila field</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory2004_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory2004-135">[135]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In August 1990, Iraqi forces <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" title="Invasion of Kuwait">invaded and annexed</a> Kuwait. After a series of failed diplomatic negotiations, the United States led a coalition to remove the Iraqi forces from Kuwait, in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>. On 26 February 1991, the coalition succeeded in driving out the Iraqi forces. As they retreated, Iraqi forces carried out a <a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_oil_fires" title="Kuwaiti oil fires">scorched earth</a> policy by setting oil wells on fire.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> During the Iraqi occupation, more than 1,000 Kuwaiti civilians were killed. In addition, more than 600 Kuwaitis went <a href="/wiki/Missing_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Missing people">missing</a> during Iraq's occupation;<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> remains of approximately 375 were found in mass graves in Iraq. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:POTD-September-5-2018.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/POTD-September-5-2018.jpg/220px-POTD-September-5-2018.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/POTD-September-5-2018.jpg/330px-POTD-September-5-2018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/POTD-September-5-2018.jpg/440px-POTD-September-5-2018.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2600" data-file-height="1733"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:POTD-September-5-2018.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> welcomes the Amir of Kuwait <a href="/wiki/Sabah_Al-Ahmad_Al-Jaber_Al-Sabah" title="Sabah Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah">Sabah Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah</a> to the White House, 2018
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In March 2003, Kuwait became the springboard for the US-led <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a>. Upon the death of the Emir Jaber in January 2006, <a href="/wiki/Saad_Al-Sabah" class="mw-redirect" title="Saad Al-Sabah">Saad Al-Sabah</a> succeeded him but was removed nine days later by the Kuwaiti parliament due to his ailing health. <a href="/wiki/Sabah_Al-Sabah" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabah Al-Sabah">Sabah Al-Sabah</a> was sworn in as Emir. </p>
      <p>From 2001 to 2009, Kuwait had the highest <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> ranking in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-kuna_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuna-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HDI2_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI2-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HDI21_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI21-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HDI1_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI1-141">[141]</a></sup> In 2005, women won the right to vote and run in elections. In 2014 and 2015, Kuwait was ranked first among Arab countries in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Gender_Gap_Report" title="Global Gender Gap Report">Global Gender Gap Report</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hgh_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hgh-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wid_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wid-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wef_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wef-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sabah_Al_Ahmad_Sea_City" title="Sabah Al Ahmad Sea City">Sabah Al Ahmad Sea City</a> was inaugurated in mid-2015.<sup id="cite_ref-twf_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twf-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Amiri_Diwan_of_Kuwait" title="Amiri Diwan of Kuwait">Amiri Diwan</a> is currently developing the new <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_National_Cultural_District" title="Kuwait National Cultural District">Kuwait National Cultural District</a> (KNCD), which comprises <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Abdullah_Al_Salem_Cultural_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikh Abdullah Al Salem Cultural Centre">Sheikh Abdullah Al Salem Cultural Centre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Jaber_Al_Ahmad_Cultural_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikh Jaber Al Ahmad Cultural Centre">Sheikh Jaber Al Ahmad Cultural Centre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Shaheed_Park" title="Al Shaheed Park">Al Shaheed Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Al_Salam_Palace_(Kuwait)" title="Al Salam Palace (Kuwait)">Al Salam Palace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kncd_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kncd-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kncd2_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kncd2-148">[148]</a></sup> With a capital cost of more than US$1&nbsp;billion, the project is one of the largest cultural investments in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-kncd_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kncd-147">[147]</a></sup> In November 2016, the Sheikh Jaber Al Ahmad Cultural Centre opened.<sup id="cite_ref-alb_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alb-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> It is the largest cultural centre in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-omnd_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omnd-150">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-opsc_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-opsc-151">[151]</a></sup> The Kuwait National Cultural District is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Global_Cultural_Districts_Network" title="Global Cultural Districts Network">Global Cultural Districts Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gcdn_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcdn-19">[19]</a></sup> In 2016 Kuwait commenced a new national development plan, Kuwait Vision 2035, including plans to diversify the economy and become less dependent on oil.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 30 September 2020, Kuwait's Crown Prince Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Nawaf_Al-Ahmad_Al-Jaber_Al-Sabah" title="Nawaf Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah">Nawaf Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah</a> became the 16th Emir of Kuwait and the successor to Emir Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Sabah_Al-Ahmad_Al-Jaber_Al-Sabah" title="Sabah Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah">Sabah Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah</a>, who died at the age of 91.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> On 7 October 2020, <a href="/wiki/Mishal_Al-Ahmad_Al-Jaber_Al-Sabah" title="Mishal Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah">Mishal Al-Ahmad Al-Jaber Al-Sabah</a> was nominated to be the Crown Prince by Emir Sheikh Nawaf. On 8 October, Kuwait's National Assembly approved Sheikh Mishal as the Crown Prince, where he took the oath of office, and pledged commitment to democracy and peace.<sup id="cite_ref-Al_Jazeera_2020_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al_Jazeera_2020-154">[154]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Kuwait" title="Geography of Kuwait">Geography of Kuwait</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Kuwait" title="Environmental issues in Kuwait">Environmental issues in Kuwait</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1008px">
       <div class="thumbinner">
        <div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kuwait_City_banner.jpg" class="image" title="Skyline of Kuwait City, capital and largest city of Kuwait"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kuwait_City_banner.jpg/1000px-Kuwait_City_banner.jpg" decoding="async" width="1000" height="143" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kuwait_City_banner.jpg/1500px-Kuwait_City_banner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kuwait_City_banner.jpg/2000px-Kuwait_City_banner.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5897" data-file-height="842"></a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kuwait_City_banner.jpg" title="File:Kuwait City banner.jpg"> </a>
         </div>Skyline of <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_City" title="Kuwait City">Kuwait City</a>, capital and largest city of Kuwait
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg/220px-Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg/330px-Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg/440px-Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="762" data-file-height="703"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Kuwait_in_November_2001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A satellite image of Kuwait reveals its desert topography.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kuwait_pol_06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Kuwait_pol_06.jpg/220px-Kuwait_pol_06.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Kuwait_pol_06.jpg/330px-Kuwait_pol_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Kuwait_pol_06.jpg/440px-Kuwait_pol_06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1009" data-file-height="1155"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kuwait_pol_06.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Kuwait shares land borders with Iraq and Saudi Arabia, and maritime borders with Iraq, Saudi Arabia, and Iran.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Located in the north-east corner of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, Kuwait is one of the smallest countries in the world in terms of land area. Kuwait lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/28th_parallel_north" title="28th parallel north">28°</a> and <a href="/wiki/31st_parallel_north" title="31st parallel north">31° N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/46th_meridian_east" title="46th meridian east">46°</a> and <a href="/wiki/49th_meridian_east" title="49th meridian east">49° E</a>. The flat, sandy <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Desert" title="Arabian Desert">Arabian Desert</a> covers most of Kuwait. Kuwait is generally low-lying, with the highest point being 306&nbsp;m (1,004&nbsp;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above sea level">above sea level</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Kuwait has <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Kuwait" title="List of islands of Kuwait">nine islands</a>, all of which, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Failaka_Island" title="Failaka Island">Failaka Island</a>, are uninhabited.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> With an area of 860&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (330&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), the <a href="/wiki/Bubiyan_Island" title="Bubiyan Island">Bubiyan</a> is the largest island in Kuwait and is connected to the rest of the country by a 2,380-metre-long (7,808&nbsp;ft) bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> 0.6% of Kuwaiti land area is considered arable<sup id="cite_ref-cia_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-3">[3]</a></sup> with sparse vegetation found along its 499-kilometre-long (310&nbsp;mi) coastline.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_City" title="Kuwait City">Kuwait City</a> is located on <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_Bay" title="Kuwait Bay">Kuwait Bay</a>, a natural deep-water harbor. </p>
      <p>Kuwait's <a href="/wiki/Burgan_field" title="Burgan field">Burgan field</a> has a total capacity of approximately 70 billion barrels (11&nbsp;billion cubic metres) of proven oil reserves. During the 1991 <a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_oil_fires" title="Kuwaiti oil fires">Kuwaiti oil fires</a>, more than 500 oil lakes were created covering a combined surface area of about 35.7&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (<span class="frac nowrap">13<span class="visualhide">&nbsp;</span><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The resulting soil contamination due to oil and soot accumulation had made eastern and south-eastern parts of Kuwait uninhabitable. Sand and oil residue had reduced large parts of the Kuwaiti desert to semi-asphalt surfaces.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The oil spills during the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> also drastically affected Kuwait's marine resources.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Kuwait" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate of Kuwait">Climate of Kuwait</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The spring season in March is warm with occasional thunderstorms. The frequent winds from the northwest are cold in winter and hot in summer. Southeasterly damp winds spring up between July and October. Hot and dry south winds prevail in spring and early summer. The shamal, a northwesterly wind common during June and July, causes dramatic sandstorms.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Summers in Kuwait are some of the hottest on earth. The highest recorded temperature was 54&nbsp;°C (129&nbsp;°F) at <a href="/wiki/Mitribah" title="Mitribah">Mitribah</a> on 21 July 2016, which is the highest temperature recorded in Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2014 Kuwait was the fourth highest country in the world in term of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions_per_capita" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita">CO2 per capita emissions</a> after <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cura%C3%A7ao" title="Curaçao">Curaçao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a> according to the World Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-test_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-163">[163]</a></sup> On average, every single inhabitant released 25.2 metric tons of CO2 in the atmosphere that year. In comparison, the world average was 5.0 tons per capita the same year. </p>
      <p>Access to <a href="/wiki/Biocapacity" title="Biocapacity">biocapacity</a> in Kuwait is lower than world average. In 2016, Kuwait had 0.59 global hectares <sup id="cite_ref-footprintdata_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footprintdata-164">[164]</a></sup> of biocapacity per person within its territory, much less than the world average of 1.6 global hectares per person.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> In 2016 Kuwait used 8.6 global hectares of biocapacity per person - their <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> of consumption. This means they use about 14.5 times as much biocapacity as Kuwait contains. As a result, Kuwait is running a biocapacity deficit.<sup id="cite_ref-footprintdata_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footprintdata-164">[164]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_parks">National parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: National parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>At present, there are five <a href="/wiki/Protected_areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Protected areas">protected areas</a> in Kuwait recognized by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>. In response to Kuwait becoming the <a href="/wiki/List_of_parties_to_the_Ramsar_Convention" title="List of parties to the Ramsar Convention">169th signatory</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ramsar_Convention" title="Ramsar Convention">Ramsar Convention</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bubiyan_Island" title="Bubiyan Island">Bubiyan Island</a>'s Mubarak al-Kabeer reserve was designated as the country's first Wetland of International Importance.<sup id="cite_ref-ramsar_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ramsar-166">[166]</a></sup> The 50,948&nbsp;ha reserve consists of small lagoons and shallow <a href="/wiki/Saltmarsh" class="mw-redirect" title="Saltmarsh">salt marshes</a> and is important as a stop-over for migrating birds on two migration routes.<sup id="cite_ref-ramsar_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ramsar-166">[166]</a></sup> The reserve is home to the world's largest breeding colony of <a href="/wiki/Crab-plover" title="Crab-plover">crab-plover</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ramsar_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ramsar-166">[166]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuwait&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Kuwait" title="Wildlife of Kuwait">Wildlife of Kuwait</a>
      </div> 
      <p>More than 363 species of birds were recorded in Kuwait, 18 species of which breed in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-bsc-eoc_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bsc-eoc-167">[167]</a></sup> Kuwait is situated at the crossroads of several major bird migration routes and between two and three million birds pass each year.<sup id="cite_ref-natstrat_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natstrat-168">[168]</a></sup> The marshes in northern Kuwait and Jahra have become increasingly important as a refuge for passage migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-natstrat_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natstrat-168">[168]</a></sup> Kuwaiti islands are important breeding areas for four species of <a href="/wiki/Tern" title="Tern">tern</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Socotra_cormorant" title="Socotra cormorant">socotra cormorant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-natstrat_168-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natstrat-168">[168]</a></sup> </p>
